     <p>
      <b>
       Thomas Alva Edison
      </b>
      (February 11, 1847 – October 18, 1931) was an American inventor and businessman. He developed many devices that greatly influenced life around the world, including the
      <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">
       phonograph
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Movie_camera" title="Movie camera">
       motion picture camera
      </a>
      , and the long-lasting, practical electric
      <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">
       light bulb
      </a>
      . Dubbed "The Wizard of Menlo Park",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wizard_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wizard-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      he was one of the first inventors to apply the principles of
      <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">
       mass production
      </a>
      and large-scale teamwork to the process of invention, and because of that, he is often credited with the creation of the first industrial
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Research_laboratory" title="Research laboratory">
       research laboratory
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walsh_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Walsh-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison was a
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_prolific_inventors" title="List of prolific inventors">
       prolific inventor
      </a>
      , holding 1,093
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Edison_patents" title="List of Edison patents">
       US patents in his name
      </a>
      , as well as many patents in the United Kingdom, France, and Germany. More significant than the number of Edison's patents was the widespread impact of his inventions:
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_light" title="Electric light">
       electric light
      </a>
      and power
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utilities" title="Utilities">
       utilities
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_recording" title="Sound recording">
       sound recording
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motion_pictures" title="Motion pictures">
       motion pictures
      </a>
      all established major new industries worldwide. Edison's inventions contributed to
      <a href="/wiki/Mass_communication" title="Mass communication">
       mass communication
      </a>
      and, in particular, telecommunications. These included a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stock_ticker" title="Stock ticker">
       stock ticker
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Voting_machine" title="Voting machine">
       mechanical vote recorder
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">
       battery
      </a>
      for an
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">
       electric car
      </a>
      , electrical power, recorded music and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motion_picture" title="Motion picture">
       motion pictures
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      His advanced work in these fields was an outgrowth of his early career as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegraph" title="Telegraph">
       telegraph
      </a>
      operator. Edison developed a system of electric-power generation and distribution
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coned1_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-coned1-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      to homes, businesses, and factories – a crucial development in the modern industrialized world. His first
      <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Street_Station" title="Pearl Street Station">
       power station
      </a>
      was on Pearl Street in
      <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">
       Manhattan
      </a>
      , New York.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coned1_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-coned1-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He has been described as America’s greatest inventor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">
       Early life
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Thomas_Edison.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1204" data-file-width="941" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Young_Thomas_Edison.jpg/200px-Young_Thomas_Edison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Young_Thomas_Edison.jpg/300px-Young_Thomas_Edison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Young_Thomas_Edison.jpg/400px-Young_Thomas_Edison.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Thomas_Edison.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Edison as a boy
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Thomas Edison was born in
      <a href="/wiki/Milan,_Ohio" title="Milan, Ohio">
       Milan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">
       Ohio
      </a>
      , and grew up in
      <a href="/wiki/Port_Huron,_Michigan" title="Port Huron, Michigan">
       Port Huron
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">
       Michigan
      </a>
      . He was the seventh and last child of Samuel Ogden Edison, Jr. (1804–1896, born in
      <a href="/wiki/Marshalltown,_Nova_Scotia" title="Marshalltown, Nova Scotia">
       Marshalltown, Nova Scotia
      </a>
      ) and Nancy Matthews Elliott (1810–1871, born in
      <a href="/wiki/Chenango_County,_New_York" title="Chenango County, New York">
       Chenango County, New York
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      His father, the son of a
      <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution)" title="Loyalist (American Revolution)">
       Loyalist
      </a>
      refugee, had moved as a boy with the family from Nova Scotia, settling in
      <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Ontario" title="Southwestern Ontario">
       southwestern Ontario
      </a>
      (then called
      <a href="/wiki/Upper_Canada" title="Upper Canada">
       Upper Canada
      </a>
      ), in a village known as Shewsbury, later
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vienna,_Ontario" title="Vienna, Ontario">
       Vienna
      </a>
      , by 1811. Samuel Jr. eventually fled Ontario because he took part in the unsuccessful
      <a href="/wiki/Rebellions_of_1837" title="Rebellions of 1837">
       Mackenzie Rebellion
      </a>
      of 1837.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      His father, Samuel Sr., had earlier fought in the
      <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">
       War of 1812
      </a>
      as captain of the First Middlesex Regiment. By contrast, Samuel Jr.'s struggle found him on the losing side, and he crossed into the United States at
      <a href="/wiki/Sarnia" title="Sarnia">
       Sarnia
      </a>
      -Port Huron. Once across the border, he found his way to Milan, Ohio. His patrilineal family line was Dutch by way of New Jersey; the surname had originally been "Edeson."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      His mother taught him at home.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Much of his education came from reading R.G. Parker's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/School_of_Natural_Philosophy" title="School of Natural Philosophy">
        School of Natural Philosophy
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Cooper_Union_for_the_Advancement_of_Science_and_Art" title="The Cooper Union for the Advancement of Science and Art">
       The Cooper Union for the Advancement of Science and Art
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison developed hearing problems at an early age. The cause of his deafness has been attributed to a bout of
      <a href="/wiki/Scarlet_fever" title="Scarlet fever">
       scarlet fever
      </a>
      during childhood and recurring untreated middle-ear infections. Around the middle of his career, Edison attributed the hearing impairment to being struck on the ears by a train conductor when his chemical laboratory in a boxcar caught fire and he was thrown off the train in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smiths_Creek,_Michigan" title="Smiths Creek, Michigan">
       Smiths Creek, Michigan
      </a>
      , along with his apparatus and chemicals. In his later years, he modified the story to say the injury occurred when the conductor, in helping him onto a moving train, lifted him by the ears.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Josephson_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Josephson-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison's family moved to
      <a href="/wiki/Port_Huron,_Michigan" title="Port Huron, Michigan">
       Port Huron, Michigan
      </a>
      , after the railroad bypassed Milan in 1854 and business declined.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Edison sold candy and newspapers on trains running from Port Huron to Detroit, and sold vegetables to supplement his income. He also studied qualitative analysis, and conducted chemical experiments on the train until an accident prohibited further work of the kind.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_CAB.7CEdison.2C_Thomas_Alva_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cite_CAB.7CEdison.2C_Thomas_Alva-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison obtained the exclusive right to sell newspapers on the road, and, with the aid of four assistants, he set in type and printed the
      <i>
       Grand Trunk Herald
      </i>
      , which he sold with his other papers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_CAB.7CEdison.2C_Thomas_Alva_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cite_CAB.7CEdison.2C_Thomas_Alva-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This began Edison's long streak of entrepreneurial ventures, as he discovered his talents as a businessman. These talents eventually led him to found 14 companies, including
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">
       General Electric
      </a>
      , which is still one of the largest
      <a href="/wiki/Public_company" title="Public company">
       publicly traded companies
      </a>
      in the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Telegrapher">
       Telegrapher
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Edison became a telegraph operator after he saved three-year-old Jimmie MacKenzie from being struck by a runaway train. Jimmie's father,
      <a href="/wiki/Station_master" title="Station master">
       station agent
      </a>
      J.U. MacKenzie of
      <a href="/wiki/Mount_Clemens,_Michigan" title="Mount Clemens, Michigan">
       Mount Clemens, Michigan
      </a>
      , was so grateful that he trained Edison as a telegraph operator. Edison's first telegraphy job away from Port Huron was at Stratford Junction,
      <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">
       Ontario
      </a>
      , on the
      <a href="/wiki/Grand_Trunk_Railway" title="Grand Trunk Railway">
       Grand Trunk Railway
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1866, at the age of 19, Edison moved to
      <a href="/wiki/Louisville,_Kentucky" title="Louisville, Kentucky">
       Louisville, Kentucky
      </a>
      , where, as an employee of
      <a href="/wiki/Western_Union" title="Western Union">
       Western Union
      </a>
      , he worked the
      <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">
       Associated Press
      </a>
      bureau
      <a href="/wiki/News_agency" title="News agency">
       news wire
      </a>
      . Edison requested the night shift, which allowed him plenty of time to spend at his two favorite pastimes—reading and experimenting. Eventually, the latter pre-occupation cost him his job. One night in 1867, he was working with a
      <a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">
       lead–acid battery
      </a>
      when he spilled
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">
       sulfuric acid
      </a>
      onto the floor. It ran between the floorboards and onto his boss's desk below. The next morning Edison was fired.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One of his mentors during those early years was a fellow telegrapher and inventor named
      <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Leonard_Pope" title="Franklin Leonard Pope">
       Franklin Leonard Pope
      </a>
      , who allowed the impoverished youth to live and work in the basement of his
      <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth,_New_Jersey" title="Elizabeth, New Jersey">
       Elizabeth, New Jersey
      </a>
      , home. Some of Edison's earliest inventions were related to telegraphy, including a stock ticker. His first patent was for the electric vote recorder, (U.S. Patent 90,646),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      which was granted on June 1, 1869.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Marriages_and_children">
       Marriages and children
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      On December 25, 1871, Edison married 16-year-old Mary Stilwell (1855–1884), whom he had met two months earlier; she was an employee at one of his shops. They had three children:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Marion Estelle Edison (1873–1965), nicknamed "Dot"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Thomas Alva Edison, Jr. (1876–1935), nicknamed "Dash"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       William Leslie Edison (1878–1937) Inventor, graduate of the Sheffield Scientific School at Yale, 1900.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Mary Edison died at age 29 on August 9, 1884, of unknown causes: possibly from a
      <a href="/wiki/Brain_tumor" title="Brain tumor">
       brain tumor
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      or a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morphine_overdose" title="Morphine overdose">
       morphine overdose
      </a>
      . Doctors frequently prescribed morphine to women in those years to treat a variety of causes, and researchers believe that some of her symptoms sounded as if they were associated with morphine poisoning.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rutgers_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rutgers-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mina_Edison_1906.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="610" data-file-width="420" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mina_Edison_1906.jpg/220px-Mina_Edison_1906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mina_Edison_1906.jpg/330px-Mina_Edison_1906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/Mina_Edison_1906.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mina_Edison_1906.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Mina Miller Edison in 1906
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On February 24, 1886, at the age of thirty-nine, Edison married the 20-year-old Mina Miller (1865–1947) in
      <a href="/wiki/Akron,_Ohio" title="Akron, Ohio">
       Akron, Ohio
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      She was the daughter of the inventor
      <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Miller_(philanthropist)" title="Lewis Miller (philanthropist)">
       Lewis Miller
      </a>
      , co-founder of the
      <a href="/wiki/Chautauqua_Institution" title="Chautauqua Institution">
       Chautauqua Institution
      </a>
      and a benefactor of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist">
       Methodist
      </a>
      charities. They also had three children together:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Madeleine Edison (1888–1979), who married
       <a href="/wiki/John_Eyre_Sloane" title="John Eyre Sloane">
        John Eyre Sloane
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edison" title="Charles Edison">
        Charles Edison
       </a>
       (1890–1969),
       <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_Jersey" title="Governor of New Jersey">
        Governor of New Jersey
       </a>
       (1941 – 1944), who took over his father's company and experimental laboratories upon his father's death.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Miller_Edison" title="Theodore Miller Edison">
        Theodore Miller Edison
       </a>
       (1898–1992), (MIT Physics 1923), credited with more than 80 patents.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Mina outlived Thomas Edison, dying on August 24, 1947.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_his_career">
       Beginning his career
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edison_and_phonograph_edit1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3327" data-file-width="2640" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Edison_and_phonograph_edit1.jpg/170px-Edison_and_phonograph_edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Edison_and_phonograph_edit1.jpg/255px-Edison_and_phonograph_edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Edison_and_phonograph_edit1.jpg/340px-Edison_and_phonograph_edit1.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edison_and_phonograph_edit1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Photograph of Edison with his phonograph (2nd model), taken in
        <a href="/wiki/Mathew_Brady" title="Mathew Brady">
         Mathew Brady
        </a>
        's Washington, DC studio in April 1878.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <table class="mbox-small-left" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;float:left">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div class="center">
         <div class="floatnone">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edison_Mary_had_lamb.ogg" title="File:Thomas Edison Mary had lamb.ogg">
           Mary Had a Little Lamb
          </a>
         </div>
         <div>
          <div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px">
           <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="15.948" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Thomas_Edison_Mary_had_lamb.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px">
            <source data-bandwidth="32831" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (33 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Thomas_Edison_Mary_had_lamb.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'>
            </source>
            <track data-dir="ltr" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="TimedText:Thomas_Edison_Mary_had_lamb.ogg.en.srt" kind="subtitles" label="English (en) subtitles" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?title=TimedText:Thomas_Edison_Mary_had_lamb.ogg.en.srt&amp;action=raw&amp;ctype=text%2Fx-srt" srclang="en" type="text/x-srt">
            </track>
           </audio>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
          Thomas Edison reciting "Mary Had a Little Lamb"
         </div>
        </div>
        <hr/>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <span>
         <i>
          Problems playing this file? See
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">
           media help
          </a>
          .
         </i>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Edison began his career as an inventor in
      <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">
       Newark, New Jersey
      </a>
      , with the automatic repeater and his other improved telegraphic devices, but the invention that first gained him notice was the
      <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">
       phonograph
      </a>
      in 1877.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This accomplishment was so unexpected by the public at large as to appear almost magical. Edison became known as "The Wizard of Menlo Park," New Jersey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wizard_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wizard-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      His first phonograph recorded on
      <a href="/wiki/Tin#Applications" title="Tin">
       tinfoil
      </a>
      around a grooved cylinder. Despite its limited
      <a href="/wiki/Sound_quality" title="Sound quality">
       sound quality
      </a>
      and that the recordings could be played only a few times, the phonograph made Edison a celebrity. Joseph Henry, president of the National Academy of Sciences and one of the most renowned electrical scientists in the US, described Edison as "the most ingenious inventor in this country... or in any other".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In April 1878, Edison travelled to Washington to demonstrate the phonograph before the National Academy of Sciences, Congressmen, Senators and US President Hayes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Post" title="Washington Post">
        Washington Post
       </a>
      </i>
      described Edison as a "
      <a href="/wiki/Genius" title="Genius">
       genius
      </a>
      " and his presentation as "a scene... that will live in history".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although Edison obtained a patent for the phonograph in 1878,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      he did little to develop it until
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">
       Alexander Graham Bell
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chichester_Bell" title="Chichester Bell">
       Chichester Bell
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sumner_Tainter" title="Charles Sumner Tainter">
       Charles Tainter
      </a>
      produced a phonograph-like device in the 1880s that used wax-coated cardboard cylinders.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Menlo_Park">
       Menlo Park
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Menlo_Park_Laboratory.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1819" data-file-width="2274" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Menlo_Park_Laboratory.JPG/250px-Menlo_Park_Laboratory.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Menlo_Park_Laboratory.JPG/375px-Menlo_Park_Laboratory.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Menlo_Park_Laboratory.JPG/500px-Menlo_Park_Laboratory.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Menlo_Park_Laboratory.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Edison's Menlo Park Laboratory, reconstructed at Greenfield Village at
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_Ford_Museum" title="Henry Ford Museum">
         Henry Ford Museum
        </a>
        in Dearborn, Michigan. (Note the organ against the back wall)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Edison's major innovation was the first industrial research lab, which was built in
      <a href="/wiki/Menlo_Park,_New_Jersey" title="Menlo Park, New Jersey">
       Menlo Park
      </a>
      , a part of Raritan Township,
      <a href="/wiki/Middlesex_County,_New_Jersey" title="Middlesex County, New Jersey">
       Middlesex County, New Jersey
      </a>
      (today named
      <a href="/wiki/Edison,_New_Jersey" title="Edison, New Jersey">
       Edison
      </a>
      in his honor). It was built with the funds from the sale of Edison's
      <a href="/wiki/Quadruplex_telegraph" title="Quadruplex telegraph">
       quadruplex telegraph
      </a>
      . After his demonstration of the telegraph, Edison was not sure that his original plan to sell it for $4,000 to $5,000 was right, so he asked Western Union to make a bid. He was surprised to hear them offer $10,000 ($209,500 in today's dollars.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ), which he gratefully accepted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The quadruplex telegraph was Edison's first big financial success, and Menlo Park became the first institution set up with the specific purpose of producing constant technological innovation and improvement. Edison was legally attributed with most of the inventions produced there, though many employees carried out research and development under his direction. His staff was generally told to carry out his directions in conducting research, and he drove them hard to produce results.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/William_Joseph_Hammer" title="William Joseph Hammer">
       William Joseph Hammer
      </a>
      , a consulting electrical engineer, began his duties as a laboratory assistant to Edison in December 1879. He assisted in experiments on the telephone, phonograph, electric railway,
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Ore-Milling_Company" title="Edison Ore-Milling Company">
       iron ore separator
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">
       electric lighting
      </a>
      , and other developing inventions. However, Hammer worked primarily on the incandescent electric lamp and was put in charge of tests and records on that device (see
      <a href="/wiki/Hammer_Historical_Collection_of_Incandescent_Electric_Lamps" title="Hammer Historical Collection of Incandescent Electric Lamps">
       Hammer Historical Collection of Incandescent Electric Lamps
      </a>
      ). In 1880, he was appointed chief engineer of the Edison Lamp Works. In his first year, the plant under General Manager
      <a href="/wiki/Francis_Robbins_Upton" title="Francis Robbins Upton">
       Francis Robbins Upton
      </a>
      turned out 50,000 lamps. According to Edison, Hammer was "a pioneer of incandescent electric lighting".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <span id="sprague">
      </span>
      <a href="/wiki/Frank_J._Sprague" title="Frank J. Sprague">
       Frank J. Sprague
      </a>
      , a competent mathematician and former
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">
       naval officer
      </a>
      , was recruited by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_H._Johnson" title="Edward H. Johnson">
       Edward H. Johnson
      </a>
      and joined the Edison organization in 1883. One of Sprague's contributions to the Edison Laboratory at Menlo Park was to expand Edison's mathematical methods. Despite the common belief that Edison did not use mathematics, analysis of his notebooks reveal that he was an astute user of mathematical analysis conducted by his assistants such as Francis Robbins Upton, for example, determining the critical parameters of his electric lighting system including lamp resistance by an analysis of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ohm%27s_Law" title="Ohm's Law">
       Ohm's Law
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joule%27s_first_law" title="Joule's first law">
       Joule's Law
      </a>
      and economics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Nearly all of Edison's patents were utility patents, which were protected for a 17-year period and included inventions or processes that are electrical, mechanical, or chemical in nature. About a dozen were
      <a href="/wiki/Design_patent" title="Design patent">
       design patents
      </a>
      , which protect an ornamental design for up to a 14-year period. As in most patents, the inventions he described were improvements over
      <a href="/wiki/Prior_art" title="Prior art">
       prior art
      </a>
      . The phonograph patent, in contrast, was unprecedented as describing the first device to record and reproduce sounds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In just over a decade, Edison's Menlo Park laboratory had expanded to occupy two city blocks. Edison said he wanted the lab to have "a stock of almost every conceivable material".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A newspaper article printed in 1887 reveals the seriousness of his claim, stating the lab contained "eight thousand kinds of chemicals, every kind of screw made, every size of needle, every kind of cord or wire, hair of humans, horses, hogs, cows, rabbits, goats, minx, camels ... silk in every texture, cocoons, various kinds of hoofs, shark's teeth, deer horns, tortoise shell ... cork, resin, varnish and oil, ostrich feathers, a peacock's tail, jet, amber, rubber, all ores ..." and the list goes on.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Over his desk, Edison displayed a placard with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Joshua_Reynolds" title="Sir Joshua Reynolds">
       Sir Joshua Reynolds
      </a>
      ' famous quotation: "There is no expedient to which a man will not resort to avoid the real labor of thinking."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This slogan was reputedly posted at several other locations throughout the facility.
     </p>
     <p>
      With Menlo Park, Edison had created the first industrial laboratory concerned with creating knowledge and then controlling its application.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_telephone_transmitter">
       Carbon telephone transmitter
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1877–78, Edison invented and developed the
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_microphone" title="Carbon microphone">
       carbon microphone
      </a>
      used in all telephones along with the Bell receiver until the 1980s. After protracted patent litigation, in 1892 a federal court ruled that Edison and not
      <a href="/wiki/Emile_Berliner" title="Emile Berliner">
       Emile Berliner
      </a>
      was the inventor of the carbon microphone. The carbon microphone was also used in radio broadcasting and public address work through the 1920s.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_light">
       Electric light
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_light_bulb" title="History of the light bulb">
       History of the light bulb
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edison_bulb.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="382" height="345" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Edison_bulb.jpg/220px-Edison_bulb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Edison_bulb.jpg/330px-Edison_bulb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Edison_bulb.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edison_bulb.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Thomas Edison's first successful light bulb model, used in public demonstration at Menlo Park, December 1879
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1878 Edison began working on a system of electrical illumination, something he hoped could compete with gas and oil based lighting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He began by tackling the problem of creating a long lasting incandescent lamp, something that would be needed for indoor use. Many earlier inventors had previously devised incandescent lamps, including
      <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">
       Alessandro Volta
      </a>
      's demonstration of a glowing wire in 1800 and inventions by
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Woodward_(inventor)" title="Henry Woodward (inventor)">
       Henry Woodward
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mathew_Evans" title="Mathew Evans">
       Mathew Evans
      </a>
      . Others who developed early and commercially impractical incandescent electric lamps included
      <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">
       Humphry Davy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/James_Bowman_Lindsay" title="James Bowman Lindsay">
       James Bowman Lindsay
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Moses_G._Farmer" title="Moses G. Farmer">
       Moses G. Farmer
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/William_E._Sawyer" title="William E. Sawyer">
       William E. Sawyer
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Swan" title="Joseph Swan">
       Joseph Swan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_G%C3%B6bel" title="Heinrich Göbel">
       Heinrich Göbel
      </a>
      . Some of these early bulbs had such flaws as an extremely short life, high expense to produce, and high
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">
       electric current
      </a>
      drawn, making them difficult to apply on a large scale commercially.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Israel-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :217–218
      </sup>
      Edison realized that in order to keep the thickness of the copper wire needed to connect a series of electric lights to an economically manageable size he would have to come up with a lamp that would draw a low amount of current. This meant the lamp would have to have a high
      <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance_and_conductance" title="Electrical resistance and conductance">
       resistance
      </a>
      and run at a relatively low voltage (around 110 volts).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After many experiments, first with
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      filaments and then with
      <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">
       platinum
      </a>
      and other metals, in the end Edison returned to a carbon filament.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first successful test was on October 22, 1879;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Israel-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :186
      </sup>
      it lasted 13.5 hours.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Edison continued to improve this design and by November 4, 1879, filed for U.S. patent 223,898 (granted on January 27, 1880) for an electric lamp using "a carbon filament or strip coiled and connected to platina contact wires".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patent898_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Patent898-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was the first commercially practical incandescent light.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although the patent described several ways of creating the carbon filament including "cotton and linen thread, wood splints, papers coiled in various ways",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patent898_51-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Patent898-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      it was not until several months after the patent was granted that Edison and his team discovered a
      <a href="/wiki/Carbonization" title="Carbonization">
       carbonized
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">
       bamboo
      </a>
      filament that could last over 1,200 hours. The idea of using this particular raw material originated from Edison's recalling his examination of a few threads from a bamboo fishing pole while relaxing on the shore of Battle Lake in the present-day state of
      <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">
       Wyoming
      </a>
      , where he and other members of a scientific team had traveled so that they could clearly observe a total eclipse of the sun on July 29, 1878, from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_Divide" title="Continental Divide">
       Continental Divide
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_bulb_Edison_2.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3120" data-file-width="2128" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Light_bulb_Edison_2.jpg/220px-Light_bulb_Edison_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Light_bulb_Edison_2.jpg/330px-Light_bulb_Edison_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Light_bulb_Edison_2.jpg/440px-Light_bulb_Edison_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_bulb_Edison_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        U.S. Patent#223898: Electric-Lamp. Issued January 27, 1880.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1878, Edison formed the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_Electric_Light_Company" title="Edison Electric Light Company">
       Edison Electric Light Company
      </a>
      in New York City with several financiers, including
      <a href="/wiki/J._P._Morgan" title="J. P. Morgan">
       J. P. Morgan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Trask" title="Spencer Trask">
       Spencer Trask
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the members of the
      <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_family" title="Vanderbilt family">
       Vanderbilt family
      </a>
      . Edison made the first public demonstration of his incandescent light bulb on December 31, 1879, in Menlo Park. It was during this time that he said: "We will make electricity so cheap that only the rich will burn candles."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SS_Columbia_Undated_Photograph.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="858" data-file-width="1357" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/SS_Columbia_Undated_Photograph.png/220px-SS_Columbia_Undated_Photograph.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/SS_Columbia_Undated_Photograph.png/330px-SS_Columbia_Undated_Photograph.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/SS_Columbia_Undated_Photograph.png/440px-SS_Columbia_Undated_Photograph.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SS_Columbia_Undated_Photograph.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Railroad_and_Navigation_Company" title="Oregon Railroad and Navigation Company">
         Oregon Railroad and Navigation Company
        </a>
        's new steamship, the
        <a href="/wiki/SS_Columbia_(1880)" title="SS Columbia (1880)">
         <i>
          Columbia
         </i>
        </a>
        , was the first commercial application for Edison's incandescent light bulb in 1880.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Villard" title="Henry Villard">
       Henry Villard
      </a>
      , president of the
      <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Railroad_and_Navigation_Company" title="Oregon Railroad and Navigation Company">
       Oregon Railroad and Navigation Company
      </a>
      , had attended Edison's 1879 demonstration. Villard quickly became impressed and requested Edison install his electric lighting system aboard his company's new steamer, the
      <a href="/wiki/SS_Columbia_(1880)" title="SS Columbia (1880)">
       <i>
        Columbia
       </i>
      </a>
      . Although hesitant at first, Edison relented and agreed to Villard's request. Following most of its completion in May 1880, the
      <i>
       Columbia
      </i>
      was sent to
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">
       New York City
      </a>
      , where Edison and his personnel installed
      <i>
       Columbia'
      </i>
      s new lighting system. Due to this, the
      <i>
       Columbia
      </i>
      became Edison's first commercial application for his incandescent light bulb. The Edison equipment was eventually removed from
      <i>
       Columbia
      </i>
      in 1895.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalton_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dalton-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Revolution_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Revolution-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_Latimer" title="Lewis Latimer">
       Lewis Latimer
      </a>
      joined the Edison Electric Light Company in 1884. Latimer had received a patent in January 1881 for the "Process of Manufacturing Carbons", an improved method for the production of carbon filaments for lightbulbs. Latimer worked as an engineer, a draftsman and an expert witness in patent litigation on electric lights.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/George_Westinghouse" title="George Westinghouse">
       George Westinghouse
      </a>
      's company bought
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_Diehl_(inventor)" title="Philip Diehl (inventor)">
       Philip Diehl's
      </a>
      competing
      <a href="/wiki/Electrodeless_lamp" title="Electrodeless lamp">
       induction lamp
      </a>
      patent rights (1882) for $25,000, forcing the holders of the Edison patent to charge a more reasonable rate for the use of the Edison patent rights and lowering the price of the electric lamp.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EDJ1929_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EDJ1929-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On October 8, 1883, the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">
       US patent office
      </a>
      ruled that Edison's patent was based on the work of
      <a href="/wiki/William_E._Sawyer" title="William E. Sawyer">
       William E. Sawyer
      </a>
      and was therefore invalid. Litigation continued for nearly six years, until October 6, 1889, when a judge ruled that Edison's electric-light improvement claim for "a filament of carbon of high resistance" was valid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      To avoid a possible court battle with Joseph Swan, whose British patent had been awarded a year before Edison's, he and Swan formed a joint company called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ediswan" title="Ediswan">
       Ediswan
      </a>
      to manufacture and market the invention in Britain.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Mahen_Theatre" title="Mahen Theatre">
       Mahen Theatre
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Brno" title="Brno">
       Brno
      </a>
      (in what is now the Czech Republic), which opened in 1882, was the first public building in the world to use Edison's electric lamps, with the installation supervised by Edison's assistant in the invention of the lamp,
      <a href="/wiki/Francis_Jehl" title="Francis Jehl">
       Francis Jehl
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In September 2010, a sculpture of three giant light bulbs was erected in Brno, in front of the theatre.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_power_distribution">
       Electric power distribution
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      After devising a commercially viable electric light bulb on October 21, 1879, Edison went on to develop an electric "
      <a href="/wiki/Public_utility" title="Public utility">
       utility
      </a>
      " designed to compete with the then existent gas lighting utilities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On December 17, 1880, he founded the
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Illuminating_Company" title="Edison Illuminating Company">
       Edison Illuminating Company
      </a>
      and during the 1880s he patented a system for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electricity_distribution" title="Electricity distribution">
       electricity distribution
      </a>
      . The company established the first investor-owned electric utility in 1882 on
      <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Street_Station" title="Pearl Street Station">
       Pearl Street Station
      </a>
      , New York City. It was on September 4, 1882, that Edison switched on his
      <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Pearl Street (Manhattan)">
       Pearl Street
      </a>
      generating station's electrical power distribution system, which provided 110 volts
      <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">
       direct current
      </a>
      (DC) to 59 customers in lower
      <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">
       Manhattan
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Earlier in the year, in January 1882, he had switched on the first steam-generating power station at
      <a href="/wiki/Holborn_Viaduct" title="Holborn Viaduct">
       Holborn Viaduct
      </a>
      in London. The DC supply system provided electricity supplies to street lamps and several private dwellings within a short distance of the station. On January 19, 1883, the first standardized incandescent electric lighting system employing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overhead_lines" title="Overhead lines">
       overhead wires
      </a>
      began service in
      <a href="/wiki/Roselle,_New_Jersey" title="Roselle, New Jersey">
       Roselle, New Jersey
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_currents">
       War of currents
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/War_of_Currents" title="War of Currents">
       War of Currents
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PyramidParthenon.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="292" data-file-width="450" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/PyramidParthenon.jpg/250px-PyramidParthenon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/PyramidParthenon.jpg/375px-PyramidParthenon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/PyramidParthenon.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PyramidParthenon.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Extravagant displays of electric lights quickly became a feature of public events, as in this picture from the 1897
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tennessee_Centennial_Exposition" title="Tennessee Centennial Exposition">
         Tennessee Centennial Exposition
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As Edison was expanding his
      <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">
       direct current
      </a>
      (DC) power delivery system he began receiving stiff competition from companies installing
      <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">
       alternating current
      </a>
      (AC) systems. From the early 1880s on AC
      <a href="/wiki/Arc_lamp" title="Arc lamp">
       arc lighting
      </a>
      systems for streets and large spaces had been an expanding business in the US. With the development of
      <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">
       transformers
      </a>
      in Europe and by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_(1886)" title="Westinghouse Electric (1886)">
       Westinghouse Electric
      </a>
      in the US in 1885-1886 it became possible to transmit AC very long distances over thinner and cheaper wires, and "step down" the voltage at the destination for distribution to users. This allowed AC to be used not only in street lighting but also in lighting for small business and domestic customers, the market Edison's patented low voltage DC incandescent lamp system had been designed to supply.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Edison's DC empire began suffering from one of its chief drawbacks: it was suitable only for the high density of customers found in large cities. Edison's DC plants could not deliver electricity to customers who were more than one mile from the plant and the short range left a patchwork of un-supplied customers in-between plants. Small cities and rural areas could not afford an Edison style system at all. This left a large part of market without electrical service and AC companies were expanding into this gap.
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison expressed views that AC was unworkable and the high voltages used were dangerous. As
      <a href="/wiki/George_Westinghouse" title="George Westinghouse">
       George Westinghouse
      </a>
      was installing his first AC systems in 1886, Thomas Edison began a pattern of striking out personally against his chief rival stating, "
      <i>
       Just as certain as death, Westinghouse will kill a customer within six months after he puts in a system of any size. He has got a new thing and it will require a great deal of experimenting to get it working practically.
      </i>
      "
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many reasons have been put forward for Edison's anti-AC stance. One notion is that the inventor may not have been able to grasp the more abstract theories behind AC and was trying to avoid developing a system he did not understand. Edison also appeared to have been worried about the high voltage from some competitor's misinstalled AC system killing customers and hurting the sales of electric power systems in general.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On top of all that was the simple fact that Edison Electric had based their entire design on low voltage DC and switching a standard after they had installed over 100 systems was, in Edison's mind, out of the question. By the end of 1887 Edison Electric was beginning to lose market share with Westinghouse, who had built 68 AC-based power stations to Edison's 121 DC-based stations. To make matters worse for Edison, the
      <a href="/wiki/Thomson-Houston_Electric_Company" title="Thomson-Houston Electric Company">
       Thomson-Houston Electric Company
      </a>
      of Lynn, Massachusetts (another AC-based competitor) had built 22 power stations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Parallel to the expanding competition between Edison and the AC companies was a rising public furor over a series of deaths in the spring of 1888 caused by pole mounted high voltage alternating current lines that turned into a media frenzy against the current and the seemingly greedy and callous lighting companies that used it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Edison took advantage of the public perception that AC was dangerous and teamed up with the self-styled New York anti-AC crusader
      <a href="/wiki/Harold_P._Brown" title="Harold P. Brown">
       Harold P. Brown
      </a>
      in a
      <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">
       propaganda
      </a>
      campaign, aiding Brown in the public electrocution of animals with AC as well as supported legislation to control and severely limit AC installations and voltages (to the point of making it an ineffective power delivery system) in what was now being referred to as a
      <a href="/wiki/War_of_Currents" title="War of Currents">
       "battle of currents"
      </a>
      . The development of the
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_chair" title="Electric chair">
       electric chair
      </a>
      was used in an attempt to portray AC as having a greater lethal potential than DC and
      <a href="/wiki/Smear_campaign" title="Smear campaign">
       smear
      </a>
      Westinghouse at the same time via Edison colluding with Brown and Westinghouse's chief AC rival, the Thomson-Houston Electric Company, to make sure the first electric chair was powered by a Westinghouse AC generator.
     </p>
     <p>
      Thomas Edison's staunch anti-AC tactics were not sitting well with his own stock holders. By the early 1890s Edison's company was generating much smaller profits than its AC rivals, and the War of Currents would come to an end in 1892 with Edison being forced out of controlling his own company. That year the financier
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J_P_Morgan" title="J P Morgan">
       J P Morgan
      </a>
      engineered a merger of Edison General Electric with Thomson-Houston that basically put the board of Thomson-Houston in charge of the new company called
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">
       General Electric
      </a>
      (dropping "Edison" from its name). General Electric now controlled three quarters of the US electrical business and would go on to compete with Westinghouse for the AC market.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_inventions_and_projects">
       Other inventions and projects
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fluoroscopy">
       Fluoroscopy
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Edison is credited with designing and producing the first commercially available
      <a href="/wiki/Fluoroscopy" title="Fluoroscopy">
       fluoroscope
      </a>
      , a machine that uses
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-rays" title="X-rays">
       X-rays
      </a>
      to take
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiographs" title="Radiographs">
       radiographs
      </a>
      . Until Edison discovered that
      <a href="/wiki/Scheelite" title="Scheelite">
       calcium tungstate
      </a>
      fluoroscopy screens produced brighter images than the barium
      <a href="/wiki/Platinocyanide" title="Platinocyanide">
       platinocyanide
      </a>
      screens originally used by
      <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_R%C3%B6ntgen" title="Wilhelm Röntgen">
       Wilhelm Röntgen
      </a>
      , the technology was capable of producing only very faint images.
     </p>
     <p>
      The fundamental design of Edison's fluoroscope is still in use today, although Edison himself abandoned the project after nearly losing his own eyesight and seriously injuring his assistant,
      <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Madison_Dally" title="Clarence Madison Dally">
       Clarence Dally
      </a>
      . Dally had made himself an enthusiastic human guinea pig for the fluoroscopy project and in the process been exposed to a poisonous dose of radiation. He later died of injuries related to the exposure. In 1903, a shaken Edison said "Don't talk to me about X-rays, I am afraid of them."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Telegraph_improvements">
       Telegraph improvements
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The key to Edison's fortunes was telegraphy. With knowledge gained from years of working as a telegraph operator, he learned the basics of electricity. This allowed him to make his early fortune with the
      <a href="/wiki/Ticker_tape" title="Ticker tape">
       stock ticker
      </a>
      , the first electricity-based broadcast system. On August 9, 1892, Edison received a patent for a two-way telegraph.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Motion_pictures">
       Motion pictures
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_1" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:320px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_2" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv/320px--Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:320px;height:240px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="39.9399" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 320 × 240 (738 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="738479" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/6f/Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv/Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv.160p.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth Ogg video (160P)" data-shorttitle="Ogg 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.ogv" data-width="214" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="161952" data-framerate="15"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'>
        <img alt="File:Leonard Cushing Kinetograph 1894.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv/220px--Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/>
        <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
         <span class="play-btn-large">
          <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
           Play media
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The June 1894 Leonard–Cushing bout. Each of the six one-minute rounds recorded by the Kinetoscope was made available to exhibitors for $22.50.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
         <a href="#cite_note-76">
          [76]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Customers who watched the final round saw Leonard score a knockdown.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Edison was also granted a patent for the motion picture camera or "Kinetograph". He did the electromechanical design, while his employee
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W._K._L._Dickson" title="W. K. L. Dickson">
       W. K. L. Dickson
      </a>
      , a photographer, worked on the photographic and optical development. Much of the credit for the invention belongs to Dickson.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_47-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Israel-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1891, Thomas Edison built a
      <a href="/wiki/Kinetoscope" title="Kinetoscope">
       Kinetoscope
      </a>
      , or peep-hole viewer. This device was installed in penny arcades, where people could watch short, simple films. The kinetograph and kinetoscope were both first publicly exhibited May 20, 1891.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In April 1896,
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Armat" title="Thomas Armat">
       Thomas Armat
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Vitascope" title="Vitascope">
       Vitascope
      </a>
      , manufactured by the Edison factory and marketed in Edison's name, was used to project motion pictures in public screenings in New York City. Later he exhibited motion pictures with voice soundtrack on cylinder recordings, mechanically synchronized with the film.
     </p>
     <p>
      Officially the kinetoscope entered Europe when the rich American Businessman
      <a href="/wiki/Irving_T._Bush" title="Irving T. Bush">
       Irving T. Bush
      </a>
      (1869–1948) bought from the Continental Commerce Company of Frank Z. Maguire and Joseph D. Baucus a dozen machines. Bush placed from October 17, 1894, the first kinetoscopes in London. At the same time the French company Kinétoscope Edison Michel et Alexis Werner bought these machines for the market in France. In the last three months of 1894, The Continental Commerce Company sold hundreds of kinetoscopes in Europe (i.e. the Netherlands and Italy). In Germany and in
      <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">
       Austria-Hungary
      </a>
      the kinetoscope was introduced by the Deutsche-österreichische-Edison-Kinetoscop Gesellschaft, founded by the Ludwig Stollwerck
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      of the Schokoladen-Süsswarenfabrik Stollwerck &amp; Co of Cologne.
     </p>
     <p>
      The first kinetoscopes arrived in Belgium at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fairs" title="Fairs">
       Fairs
      </a>
      in early 1895. The Edison's Kinétoscope Français, a Belgian company, was founded in Brussels on January 15, 1895, with the rights to sell the kinetoscopes in Monaco, France and the French colonies. The main investors in this company were Belgian industrialists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Imdb.com_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Imdb.com-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On May 14, 1895, the Edison's Kinétoscope Belge was founded in Brussels. The businessman Ladislas-Victor Lewitzki, living in London but active in Belgium and France, took the initiative in starting this business. He had contacts with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leon_Gaumont" title="Leon Gaumont">
       Leon Gaumont
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Mutoscope_and_Biograph" title="American Mutoscope and Biograph">
       American Mutoscope and Biograph
      </a>
      Co. In 1898 he also became a shareholder of the Biograph and Mutoscope Company for France.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Imdb.com_79-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Imdb.com-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Studios" title="Edison Studios">
       Edison's film studio
      </a>
      made close to 1,200 films. The majority of the productions were short films showing everything from acrobats to parades to fire calls including titles such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Fred_Ott%27s_Sneeze" title="Fred Ott's Sneeze">
        Fred Ott's Sneeze
       </a>
      </i>
      (1894),
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Kiss_(1896_film)" title="The Kiss (1896 film)">
        The Kiss
       </a>
      </i>
      (1896),
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Train_Robbery_(1903_film)" title="The Great Train Robbery (1903 film)">
        The Great Train Robbery
       </a>
      </i>
      (1903),
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Adventures_in_Wonderland_(1910_film)" title="Alice's Adventures in Wonderland (1910 film)">
        Alice's Adventures in Wonderland
       </a>
      </i>
      (1910), and the first
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Frankenstein_(1910_film)" title="Frankenstein (1910 film)">
        Frankenstein
       </a>
      </i>
      film in 1910. In 1903, when the owners of
      <a href="/wiki/Luna_Park,_Coney_Island_(1903)" title="Luna Park, Coney Island (1903)">
       Luna Park, Coney Island
      </a>
      announced they would execute
      <a href="/wiki/Topsy_(elephant)" title="Topsy (elephant)">
       Topsy the elephant
      </a>
      by strangulation, poisoning, and electrocution (with the electrocution part ultimately killing the elephant), Edison Manufacturing sent a crew to film it, releasing it that same year with the title
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Electrocuting_an_Elephant" title="Electrocuting an Elephant">
        Electrocuting an Elephant
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
       <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_3" style="width:240px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:640px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_4" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/A_Day_with_Thomas_Edison_%281922%29.webm/640px--A_Day_with_Thomas_Edison_%281922%29.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:640px;height:360px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="1124.126" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="A_Day_with_Thomas_Edison_(1922).webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/55/A_Day_with_Thomas_Edison_%281922%29.webm/A_Day_with_Thomas_Edison_%281922%29.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm;
