        <img alt="File:A Day with Thomas Edison (1922).webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/A_Day_with_Thomas_Edison_%281922%29.webm/240px-seek%3D1-A_Day_with_Thomas_Edison_%281922%29.webm.jpg" style="width:240px;height:135px"/>
        <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/55/A_Day_with_Thomas_Edison_%281922%29.webm" target="new" title="Play media">
         <span class="play-btn-large">
          <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
           Play media
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Day_with_Thomas_Edison_(1922).webm" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         A Day with Thomas Edison
        </i>
        (1922)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As the film business expanded competing exhibitors routinely copied and exhibited each other's films.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      To better protect the copyrights on his films, Edison deposited prints of them on long strips of
      <a href="/wiki/Photographic_paper" title="Photographic paper">
       photographic paper
      </a>
      with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._copyright_office" title="U.S. copyright office">
       U.S. copyright office
      </a>
      . Many of these paper prints survived longer and in better condition than the actual films of that era.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1908, Edison started the
      <a href="/wiki/Motion_Picture_Patents_Company" title="Motion Picture Patents Company">
       Motion Picture Patents Company
      </a>
      , which was a conglomerate of nine major film studios (commonly known as the Edison Trust). Thomas Edison was the first honorary fellow of the
      <a href="/wiki/Acoustical_Society_of_America" title="Acoustical Society of America">
       Acoustical Society of America
      </a>
      , which was founded in 1929.
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison said his favorite movie was
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_a_Nation" title="The Birth of a Nation">
        The Birth of a Nation
       </a>
      </i>
      . He thought that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talkies" title="Talkies">
       talkies
      </a>
      had "spoiled everything" for him. "There isn't any good acting on the screen. They concentrate on the voice now and have forgotten how to act. I can sense it more than you because I am deaf."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-condensed1042_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-condensed1042-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      His favorite stars were
      <a href="/wiki/Mary_Pickford" title="Mary Pickford">
       Mary Pickford
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Clara_Bow" title="Clara Bow">
       Clara Bow
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">
       Mining
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Starting in the late 1870s, Thomas Edison became interested and involved with mining. There was a scarcity of high-grade iron ore on the east coast of the United States and so Edison instead tried to mine low-grade ore. Edison would go on to develop a number of rollers and crushers that could pulverize rocks up to 10 tons. The dust would then be sent between three giant magnets that would pull the iron ore from the dust. Despite the failure of his mining company, the
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Ore-Milling_Company" title="Edison Ore-Milling Company">
       Edison Ore Milling Company
      </a>
      , Edison would go on to use some of the materials and equipment to begin producing cement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1901, Edison visited an industrial exhibition in the
      <a href="/wiki/Greater_Sudbury" title="Greater Sudbury">
       Sudbury
      </a>
      area in Ontario, Canada and thought nickel and cobalt deposits there could be used in his production of electrical equipment. He returned as a mining prospector, and is credited with the original discovery of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falconbridge,_Greater_Sudbury,_Ontario" title="Falconbridge, Greater Sudbury, Ontario">
       Falconbridge
      </a>
      ore body. His attempts to mine the ore body were not successful, however, and he abandoned his mining claim in 1903.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A street in Falconbridge, as well as the
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Building_(Falconbridge)" title="Edison Building (Falconbridge)">
       Edison Building
      </a>
      , which served as the head office of
      <a href="/wiki/Falconbridge_Ltd." title="Falconbridge Ltd.">
       Falconbridge Mines
      </a>
      , are named for him.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Rubber">
       Rubber
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EFWE_2008.04.01_.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1999" data-file-width="1350" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/EFWE_2008.04.01_.jpg/220px-EFWE_2008.04.01_.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/EFWE_2008.04.01_.jpg/330px-EFWE_2008.04.01_.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/EFWE_2008.04.01_.jpg/440px-EFWE_2008.04.01_.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EFWE_2008.04.01_.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        From Left to Right: Henry Ford, Thomas Edison, and Harvey Firestone, the three partners of the Edison Botanic Research Corporation.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Edison became concerned with America's reliance on foreign supply of rubber and was determined to find a native supply of rubber. He partnered with Harvey Firestone and
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">
       Henry Ford
      </a>
      (all three contributing $25,000 each) to create the Edison Botanic Research Corp. in 1927 and constructed a laboratory in Fort Myers, Florida the following year. Edison did the majority of the research and planting, sending results and sample rubber residues to his West Orange Lab. Edison employed a two-part
      <a href="/wiki/Acid-base_extraction" title="Acid-base extraction">
       Acid-base extraction
      </a>
      , to derive latex from the plant material after it was dried and crushed to a powder.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      After testing 17,000 plant samples, he eventually found an adequate source in the Goldenrod plant.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="West_Orange_and_Fort_Myers_.281886.E2.80.931931.29">
       West Orange and Fort Myers (1886–1931)
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edison_battery_exhibit,_1915.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="500" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Edison_battery_exhibit%2C_1915.jpg/220px-Edison_battery_exhibit%2C_1915.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Edison_battery_exhibit%2C_1915.jpg/330px-Edison_battery_exhibit%2C_1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Edison_battery_exhibit%2C_1915.jpg/440px-Edison_battery_exhibit%2C_1915.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edison_battery_exhibit,_1915.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Thomas A. Edison Industries Exhibit, Primary Battery section, 1915
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Edison moved from Menlo Park after the death of his first wife, Mary, in 1884, and purchased a home known as "
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison_National_Historical_Park" title="Thomas Edison National Historical Park">
       Glenmont
      </a>
      " in 1886 as a wedding gift for his second wife, Mina, in
      <a href="/wiki/Llewellyn_Park" title="Llewellyn Park">
       Llewellyn Park
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/West_Orange,_New_Jersey" title="West Orange, New Jersey">
       West Orange, New Jersey
      </a>
      . In 1885, Thomas Edison had bought property in
      <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myers,_Florida" title="Fort Myers, Florida">
       Fort Myers
      </a>
      , Florida, and built what was later called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seminole_Lodge_(Thomas_Edison)" title="Seminole Lodge (Thomas Edison)">
       Seminole Lodge
      </a>
      as a winter retreat. Edison and Mina spent many winters at their home in Fort Myers, and Edison tried to find a domestic source of natural rubber.
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison's work on rubber took place largely at his botanic research laboratory in Fort Myers, which has now been designated as a National Historic Chemical Landmark.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The laboratory was built after Thomas Edison, Henry Ford, and Harvey Firestone pulled together $75,000 to form the Edison Botanical Research Corporation. Initially, only Ford and Firestone were to contributed monetary funds to the project while Edison did all the research. Edison, however, wished to contribute $25,000 as well. After testing over 17,000 plant species, Edison decided on Solidago leavenworthii, also known as Leavenworth's Goldenrod. The plant, which normally grows roughly 3–4 feet tall with a 5% latex yield was adapted by Edison through cross-breeding to produce plants twice the size and with a latex yield of 12%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In October 1931, Edison reported that he was close to finding a domestic source of rubber but died in the same month. His brother-in-law, John Miller, would take over management of the project until it was shuttered in 1936.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years">
       Final years
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="862" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg/220px-Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg/330px-Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg/440px-Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">
         Henry Ford
        </a>
        , Thomas Edison, and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvey_Firestone" title="Harvey Firestone">
         Harvey Firestone
        </a>
        , respectively.
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ft._Myers" title="Ft. Myers">
         Ft. Myers
        </a>
        , Florida, February 11, 1929
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">
       Henry Ford
      </a>
      , the automobile magnate, later lived a few hundred feet away from Edison at his winter retreat in Fort Myers. Ford once worked as an engineer for the Edison Illuminating Company of Detroit and met Edison at a convention of affiliated Edison illuminating companies in Brooklyn, NY in 1896. Edison was impressed with Fords internal combustion engine automobile and encouraged its developments. They were friends until Edison's death. Edison and Ford undertook annual motor camping trips from 1914 to 1924. Harvey Firestone and John Burroughs also participated.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1928, Edison joined the Fort Myers
      <a href="/wiki/Civitan_International" title="Civitan International">
       Civitan Club
      </a>
      . He believed strongly in the organization, writing that "The Civitan Club is doing things—big things—for the community, state, and nation, and I certainly consider it an honor to be numbered in its ranks."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He was an active member in the club until his death, sometimes bringing Henry Ford to the club's meetings.
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison was active in business right up to the end. Just months before his death, the
      <a href="/wiki/Delaware,_Lackawanna_and_Western_Railroad" title="Delaware, Lackawanna and Western Railroad">
       Lackawanna Railroad
      </a>
      inaugurated suburban electric train service from
      <a href="/wiki/Hoboken,_New_Jersey" title="Hoboken, New Jersey">
       Hoboken
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Montclair,_New_Jersey" title="Montclair, New Jersey">
       Montclair
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Dover,_New_Jersey" title="Dover, New Jersey">
       Dover
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Gladstone,_New_Jersey" title="Gladstone, New Jersey">
       Gladstone, New Jersey
      </a>
      . Electrical transmission for this service was by means of an overhead catenary system using direct current, which Edison had championed. Despite his frail condition, Edison was at the throttle of the first electric MU (Multiple-Unit) train to depart Lackawanna Terminal in Hoboken in September 1930, driving the train the first mile through Hoboken yard on its way to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Orange,_NJ" title="South Orange, NJ">
       South Orange
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland_2001_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Holland_2001-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      This fleet of cars would serve commuters in northern New Jersey for the next 54 years until their retirement in 1984. A plaque commemorating Edison's inaugural ride can be seen today in the waiting room of Lackawanna Terminal in Hoboken, which is presently operated by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Transit" title="New Jersey Transit">
       New Jersey Transit
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland_2001_90-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Holland_2001-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison was said to have been influenced by a popular
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_faddism" title="Food faddism">
       fad diet
      </a>
      in his last few years; "the only liquid he consumed was a pint of milk every three hours".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_47-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Israel-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He is reported to have believed this diet would restore his health. However, this tale is doubtful. In 1930, the year before Edison died, Mina said in an interview about him, "correct eating is one of his greatest hobbies." She also said that during one of his periodic "great scientific adventures", Edison would be up at 7:00, have breakfast at 8:00, and be rarely home for lunch or dinner, implying that he continued to have all three.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-condensed1042_82-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-condensed1042-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison became the owner of his
      <a href="/wiki/Milan,_Ohio" title="Milan, Ohio">
       Milan, Ohio
      </a>
      , birthplace in 1906. On his last visit, in 1923, he was reportedly shocked to find his old home still lit by lamps and candles.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Death">
       Death
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Edison died of complications of diabetes on October 18, 1931, in his home, "Glenmont" in
      <a href="/wiki/Llewellyn_Park" title="Llewellyn Park">
       Llewellyn Park
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/West_Orange,_New_Jersey" title="West Orange, New Jersey">
       West Orange, New Jersey
      </a>
      , which he had purchased in 1886 as a wedding gift for Mina. He is buried behind the home.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison's last breath is reportedly contained in a test tube at the
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">
       Henry Ford Museum
      </a>
      . Ford reportedly convinced
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edison" title="Charles Edison">
       Charles Edison
      </a>
      to seal a test tube of air in the inventor's room shortly after his death, as a memento. A plaster
      <a href="/wiki/Death_mask" title="Death mask">
       death mask
      </a>
      was also made.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mina died in 1947.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_politics.2C_religion_and_metaphysics">
       Views on politics, religion and metaphysics
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Historian Paul Israel has characterized Edison as a "
      <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">
       freethinker
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_47-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Israel-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Edison was heavily influenced by
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">
       Thomas Paine
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Reason" title="The Age of Reason">
        The Age of Reason
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_47-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Israel-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Edison defended Paine's "scientific
      <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">
       deism
      </a>
      ", saying, "He has been called an
      <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">
       atheist
      </a>
      , but atheist he was not. Paine believed in a supreme intelligence, as representing the idea which other men often express by the name of deity."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_47-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Israel-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In an October 2, 1910, interview in the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times_Magazine" title="New York Times Magazine">
        New York Times Magazine
       </a>
       ,
      </i>
      Edison stated:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       Nature is what we know. We do not know the gods of religions. And nature is not kind, or merciful, or loving. If God made me — the fabled God of the three qualities of which I spoke: mercy, kindness, love — He also made the fish I catch and eat. And where do His mercy, kindness, and love for that fish come in? No; nature made us — nature did it all — not the gods of the religions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Edison was accused of being an atheist for those remarks, and although he did not allow himself to be drawn into the controversy publicly, he clarified himself in a private letter:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       You have misunderstood the whole article, because you jumped to the conclusion that it denies the existence of God. There is no such denial, what you call God I call Nature, the Supreme intelligence that rules matter. All the article states is that it is doubtful in my opinion if our intelligence or soul or whatever one may call it lives hereafter as an entity or disperses back again from whence it came, scattered amongst the cells of which we are made.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_47-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Israel-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      He also stated, "I do not believe in the God of the theologians; but that there is a Supreme Intelligence I do not doubt."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Nonviolence" title="Nonviolence">
       Nonviolence
      </a>
      was key to Edison's moral views, and when asked to serve as a naval consultant for World War I, he specified he would work only on defensive weapons and later noted, "I am proud of the fact that I never invented weapons to kill." Edison's philosophy of nonviolence extended to animals as well, about which he stated: "Nonviolence leads to the highest ethics, which is the goal of all evolution. Until we stop harming all other living beings, we are still savages."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He was a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetarian" title="Vegetarian">
       vegetarian
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but not a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegan" title="Vegan">
       vegan
      </a>
      in actual practice, at least near the end of his life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Israel_47-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Israel-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1920, Edison set off a media sensation when he told
      <a href="/wiki/B._C._Forbes" title="B. C. Forbes">
       B. C. Forbes
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Magazine" title="American Magazine">
       American Magazine
      </a>
      that he was working on a "spirit phone" to allow communication with the dead, a story which other newspapers and magazines repeated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Edison later disclaimed the idea, telling the New York Times in 1926 that "I really had nothing to tell him, but I hated to disappoint him so I thought up this story about communicating with spirits, but it was all a joke."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_money">
       Views on money
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Thomas Edison was an advocate for monetary reform in the United States. He was ardently opposed to the gold standard and debt-based money. Famously, he was quoted in the New York Times stating "Gold is a relic of Julius Caesar, and
      <a href="/wiki/Interest" title="Interest">
       interest
      </a>
      is an invention of Satan."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-query.nytimes.com_101-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-query.nytimes.com-101">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the same article, he expounded upon the absurdity of a monetary system in which the taxpayer of the United States, in need of a loan, be compelled to pay in return perhaps double the principal, or even greater sums, due to interest. His basic point was that if the Government can produce debt-based money, it could equally as well produce money that was a credit to the taxpayer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-query.nytimes.com_101-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-query.nytimes.com-101">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      He thought at length about the subject of money over 1921 and 1922. In May 1922, he published a proposal, entitled "A Proposed Amendment to the Federal Reserve Banking System".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In it, he detailed an explanation of a commodity-backed currency, in which the Federal Reserve would issue interest-free currency to farmers, based on the value of commodities they produced. During a publicity tour that he took with friend and fellow inventor,
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">
       Henry Ford
      </a>
      , he spoke publicly about his desire for monetary reform. For insight, he corresponded with prominent academic and banking professionals. In the end, however, Edison's proposals failed to find support, and were eventually abandoned.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">
       Awards
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4761" data-file-width="5778" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Archibald_Anderson_-_Thomas_Alva_Edison_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Portrait of Edison by
        <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Archibald_Anderson" title="Abraham Archibald Anderson">
         Abraham Archibald Anderson
        </a>
        (1890),
        <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_States)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United States)">
         National Portrait Gallery
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/President_of_France" title="President of France">
       President
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">
       Third French Republic
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jules_Gr%C3%A9vy" title="Jules Grévy">
       Jules Grévy
      </a>
      , on the recommendation of his
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_(France)" title="Minister of Foreign Affairs (France)">
       Minister of Foreign Affairs
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Jules_Barth%C3%A9lemy-Saint-Hilaire" title="Jules Barthélemy-Saint-Hilaire">
       Jules Barthélemy-Saint-Hilaire
      </a>
      and with the presentations of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_of_Posts,_Telegraphs,_and_Telephones_(France)" title="Minister of Posts, Telegraphs, and Telephones (France)">
       Minister of Posts and Telegraphs
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Louis_Adolphe_Cochery" title="Louis Adolphe Cochery">
       Louis Cochery
      </a>
      , designated Edison with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%A9gion_d%27honneur#The_Order_and_other_countries" title="Légion d'honneur">
       <i>
        distinction
       </i>
      </a>
      of an
      <i>
       '
       <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">
        Officer of the Legion of Honour
       </a>
       '
      </i>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%A9gion_d%27honneur" title="Légion d'honneur">
       Légion d'honneur
      </a>
      ) by decree on November 10, 1881;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He also named a Chevalier in 1879, and a Commander in 1889.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BIO-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1887, Edison won the
      <a href="/wiki/Matteucci_Medal" title="Matteucci Medal">
       Matteucci Medal
      </a>
      . In 1890, he was elected a member of the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences">
       Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_City_Council" title="Philadelphia City Council">
       Philadelphia City Council
      </a>
      named Edison the recipient of the
      <a href="/wiki/John_Scott_Medal" title="John Scott Medal">
       John Scott Medal
      </a>
      in 1889.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_106-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BIO-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1899, Edison was awarded the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Longstreth_Medal" title="Edward Longstreth Medal">
       Edward Longstreth Medal
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Franklin_Institute" title="The Franklin Institute">
       The Franklin Institute
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LongstrethMedal_Laureates_107-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LongstrethMedal_Laureates-107">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      He was named an
      <i>
       Honorable Consulting Engineer
      </i>
      at the
      <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase_Exposition" title="Louisiana Purchase Exposition">
       Louisiana Purchase Exposition
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/World%27s_fair" title="World's fair">
       World's fair
      </a>
      in 1904.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_106-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-BIO-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1908, Edison received the American Association of Engineering Societies
      <a href="/wiki/John_Fritz_Medal" title="John Fritz Medal">
       John Fritz Medal
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_106-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-BIO-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1915, Edison was awarded
      <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Medal" title="Franklin Medal">
       Franklin Medal
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Franklin_Institute" title="The Franklin Institute">
       The Franklin Institute
      </a>
      for discoveries contributing to the foundation of industries and the well-being of the human race.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        [108]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1920, The
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">
       United States Navy
      </a>
      department awarded him the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navy_Distinguished_Service_Medal" title="Navy Distinguished Service Medal">
       Navy Distinguished Service Medal
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_106-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-BIO-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1923, The
      <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Electrical_Engineers" title="American Institute of Electrical Engineers">
       American Institute of Electrical Engineers
      </a>
      created the Edison Medal and he was its first recipient.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_106-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-BIO-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1927, he was granted membership in the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">
       National Academy of Sciences
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_106-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-BIO-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On May 29, 1928, Edison received the
      <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">
       Congressional Gold Medal
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIO_106-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-BIO-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1983, the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">
       United States Congress
      </a>
      , pursuant to Senate Joint Resolution 140 (Public Law 97—198), designated February 11, Edison's birthday, as National
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inventor%27s_Day" title="Inventor's Day">
       Inventor's Day
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        [109]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">
        Life
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine (USA), in a special double issue in 1997, placed Edison first in the list of the "100 Most Important People in the Last 1000 Years", noting that the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_bulb" title="Light bulb">
       light bulb
      </a>
      he promoted "lit up the world". In the 2005 television series
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Greatest_American" title="The Greatest American">
        The Greatest American
       </a>
      </i>
      , he was voted by viewers as the fifteenth-greatest.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2008, Edison was inducted in the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Hall_of_Fame" title="New Jersey Hall of Fame">
       New Jersey Hall of Fame
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2010, Edison was honored with a
      <a href="/wiki/Technical_Grammy_Award" title="Technical Grammy Award">
       Technical Grammy Award
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2011, Edison was inducted into the
      <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneur_Walk_of_Fame" title="Entrepreneur Walk of Fame">
       Entrepreneur Walk of Fame
      </a>
      , and named a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Floridian" title="Great Floridian">
       Great Floridian
      </a>
      by the Florida Governor and Cabinet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        [110]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tributes">
       Tributes
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Places_and_people_named_for_Edison">
       Places and people named for Edison
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1391" data-file-width="1278" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg/220px-Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg/330px-Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg/440px-Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edison_3c_1947_issue_U.S._stamp.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Thomas Edison commemorative stamp, issued on the 100th anniversary of his birth in 1947
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Several places have been named after Edison, most notably the town of
      <a href="/wiki/Edison,_New_Jersey" title="Edison, New Jersey">
       Edison, New Jersey
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison_State_University" title="Thomas Edison State University">
       Thomas Edison State University
      </a>
      , nationally known for adult learners, is in
      <a href="/wiki/Trenton,_New_Jersey" title="Trenton, New Jersey">
       Trenton, New Jersey
      </a>
      . Two community colleges are named for him:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_State_College" title="Edison State College">
       Edison State College
      </a>
      (now
      <a href="/wiki/Florida_SouthWestern_State_College" title="Florida SouthWestern State College">
       Florida SouthWestern State College
      </a>
      ) in Fort Myers, Florida, and Edison Community College in
      <a href="/wiki/Piqua,_Ohio" title="Piqua, Ohio">
       Piqua, Ohio
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        [111]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are numerous high schools named after Edison (see
      <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Edison_High_School_(disambiguation)" title="Edison High School (disambiguation)">
       Edison High School
      </a>
      ) and other schools including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Edison_Middle_School" title="Thomas A. Edison Middle School">
       Thomas A. Edison Middle School
      </a>
      . Footballer
      <a href="/wiki/Pel%C3%A9" title="Pelé">
       Pelé
      </a>
      's father originally named him Edson, as a tribute to the inventor of the light bulb, but the name was incorrectly listed on his birth certificate as "Edison".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        [112]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The small town of
      <a href="/wiki/Alva,_Florida" title="Alva, Florida">
       Alva
      </a>
      just east of Fort Myers took Edison's middle name.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1883, the City Hotel in
      <a href="/wiki/Sunbury,_Pennsylvania" title="Sunbury, Pennsylvania">
       Sunbury, Pennsylvania
      </a>
      was the first building to be lit with Edison's three-wire system. The hotel was renamed The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hotel_Edison_(Sunbury,_Pennsylvania)" title="Hotel Edison (Sunbury, Pennsylvania)">
       Hotel Edison
      </a>
      upon Edison's return to the city on 1922.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sunbury_113-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sunbury-113">
        [113]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Lake_Thomas_A_Edison" title="Lake Thomas A Edison">
       Lake Thomas A Edison
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">
       California
      </a>
      was named after Edison to mark the 75th anniversary of the
      <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">
       incandescent light bulb
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sce_114-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sce-114">
        [114]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Edison was on hand to turn on the lights at the
      <a href="/wiki/Hotel_Edison" title="Hotel Edison">
       Hotel Edison
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">
       New York City
      </a>
      when it opened in 1931.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hotel_Edison_FAQ_115-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hotel_Edison_FAQ-115">
        [115]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Three bridges around the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      have been named in Edison's honor: the
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Bridge_(New_Jersey)" title="Edison Bridge (New Jersey)">
       Edison Bridge in New Jersey
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        [116]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Bridge_(Florida)" title="Edison Bridge (Florida)">
       Edison Bridge in Florida
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        [117]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Bridge_(Ohio)" title="Edison Bridge (Ohio)">
       Edison Bridge in Ohio
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        [118]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In space, his name is commemorated in
      <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
       asteroid
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/742_Edisona" title="742 Edisona">
       742 Edisona
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Museums_and_memorials">
       Museums and memorials
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edison_1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="695" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Thomas_Edison_1.jpg/220px-Thomas_Edison_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Thomas_Edison_1.jpg/330px-Thomas_Edison_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Thomas_Edison_1.jpg/440px-Thomas_Edison_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edison_1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Statue of young Thomas Edison by the railroad tracks in Port Huron, Michigan.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In West Orange, New Jersey, the 13.5 acres (5.5 hectares) Glenmont estate is maintained and operated by the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">
       National Park Service
      </a>
      as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_National_Historic_Site" title="Edison National Historic Site">
       Edison National Historic Site
      </a>
      , as is his nearby laboratory and workshops including the reconstructed Black Maria- the world's first movie studio.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        [119]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Alva_Edison_Memorial_Tower_and_Museum" title="Thomas Alva Edison Memorial Tower and Museum">
       Thomas Alva Edison Memorial Tower and Museum
      </a>
      is in the town of Edison, New Jersey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        [120]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Beaumont,_Texas" title="Beaumont, Texas">
       Beaumont, Texas
      </a>
      , there is an
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Museum" title="Edison Museum">
       Edison Museum
      </a>
      , though Edison never visited there.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        [121]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Port_Huron_Museum" title="Port Huron Museum">
       Port Huron Museum
      </a>
      , in
      <a href="/wiki/Port_Huron,_Michigan" title="Port Huron, Michigan">
       Port Huron, Michigan
      </a>
      , restored the original depot that Thomas Edison worked out of as a young newsbutcher. The depot has been named the
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison_Depot_Museum" title="Thomas Edison Depot Museum">
       Thomas Edison Depot Museum
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        [122]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The town has many Edison historical landmarks, including the graves of Edison's parents, and a monument along the
      <a href="/wiki/St._Clair_River" title="St. Clair River">
       St. Clair River
      </a>
      . Edison's influence can be seen throughout this city of 32,000.
     </p>
     <p>
      In Detroit, the Edison Memorial Fountain in
      <a href="/wiki/Grand_Circus_Park_Historic_District" title="Grand Circus Park Historic District">
       Grand Circus Park
      </a>
      was created to honor his achievements. The limestone fountain was dedicated October 21, 1929, the fiftieth anniversary of the creation of the lightbulb.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        [123]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the same night,
      <a href="/wiki/The_Henry_Ford" title="The Henry Ford">
       The Edison Institute
      </a>
      was dedicated in nearby
      <a href="/wiki/Dearborn,_Michigan" title="Dearborn, Michigan">
       Dearborn
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      He was inducted into the
      <a href="/wiki/Automotive_Hall_of_Fame" title="Automotive Hall of Fame">
       Automotive Hall of Fame
      </a>
      in 1969.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Companies_bearing_Edison.27s_name">
       Companies bearing Edison's name
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%AD%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BD_%D0%A2%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%81_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE_%D0%96%D0%97%D0%9B.JPG">

       </a>

      <li>
       Edison General Electric, merged with

        Thomson-Houston Electric Company
       </a>
       to form
       <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">
        General Electric
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Edison" title="Commonwealth Edison">
        Commonwealth Edison
       </a>
       , now part of
       <a href="/wiki/Exelon" title="Exelon">
        Exelon
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Consolidated_Edison" title="Consolidated Edison">
        Consolidated Edison
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_International" title="Edison International">
        Edison International
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detroit_Edison" title="Detroit Edison">
        Detroit Edison
       </a>
       , a unit of
       <a href="/wiki/DTE_Energy" title="DTE Energy">
        DTE Energy
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_S.p.A." title="Edison S.p.A.">
        Edison S.p.A.
       </a>
       , a unit of Italenergia
      </li>
      <li>
       Trade association the
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Electric_Institute" title="Edison Electric Institute">
        Edison Electric Institute
       </a>
       , a lobbying and research group for investor-owned utilities in the United States
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Ore-Milling_Company" title="Edison Ore-Milling Company">
        Edison Ore-Milling Company

        Southern California Edison
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_named_in_honor_of_Edison">
       Awards named in honor of Edison
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_Medal" title="Edison Medal">
       Edison Medal
      </a>
      was created on February 11, 1904, by a group of Edison's friends and associates. Four years later the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Electrical_Engineers" title="American Institute of Electrical Engineers">
       American Institute of Electrical Engineers
      </a>
      (AIEE), later
      <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Electrical_and_Electronics_Engineers" title="Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers">
       IEEE
      </a>
      , entered into an agreement with the group to present the medal as its highest award. The first medal was presented in 1909 to
      <a href="/wiki/Elihu_Thomson" title="Elihu Thomson">
       Elihu Thomson
      </a>
      . It is the oldest award in the area of
      <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">
       electrical and electronics engineering
      </a>
      , and is presented annually "for a career of meritorious achievement in electrical science, electrical engineering or the electrical arts."
     </p>
     <p>
      In the Netherlands, the major music awards are named the
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Award" title="Edison Award">
       Edison Award
      </a>
      after him. The award is an annual Dutch music prize, awarded for outstanding achievements in the music industry, and is one of the oldest music awards in the world, having been presented since 1960.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Mechanical_Engineers" title="American Society of Mechanical Engineers">
       American Society of Mechanical Engineers
      </a>
      concedes the Thomas A. Edison Patent Award to individual patents since 2000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        [124]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_items_named_after_Edison">
       Other items named after Edison
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">
       United States Navy
      </a>
      named the
      <a href="/wiki/USS_Edison_(DD-439)" title="USS Edison (DD-439)">
       USS
       <i>
        Edison
       </i>
       (DD-439)
      </a>
      , a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gleaves_class_destroyer" title="Gleaves class destroyer">
       Gleaves class destroyer
      </a>
      , in his honor in 1940. The ship was decommissioned a few months after the end of World War II.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        [125]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1962, the Navy commissioned
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Thomas_A._Edison_(SSBN-610)" title="USS Thomas A. Edison (SSBN-610)">
       USS
       <i>
        Thomas A. Edison
       </i>
       (SSBN-610)
      </a>
      , a fleet ballistic missile nuclear-powered submarine.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        [126]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">
       In popular culture
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison_in_popular_culture" title="Thomas Edison in popular culture">
       Thomas Edison in popular culture
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Thomas Edison has appeared in
      <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">
       popular culture
      </a>
      as a character in novels, films, comics and video games. His prolific inventing helped make him an icon and he has made appearances in popular culture during his lifetime down to the present day. Edison is also portrayed in popular culture as an adversary of
      <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">
       Nikola Tesla
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      "Camping with Henry and Tom", a fictional play based on Edison's camping trips with Henry Ford, written by Mark St.Gemain. First presented at Lucille Lortel Theatre, New York, February 20, 1995.
     </p>
     <p>
      On February 11, 2011, on Thomas Edison's 164th birthday,
      <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">
       Google
      </a>
      's homepage featured an animated
      <a href="/wiki/Google_Doodle" title="Google Doodle">
       Google Doodle
      </a>
      commemorating his many inventions. When the cursor was hovered over the doodle, a series of mechanisms seemed to move, causing a lightbulb to glow.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        [127]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_people_who_worked_for_Edison">
       List of people who worked for Edison
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The following is a list of people who worked for Thomas Edison in his laboratories at Menlo Park or West Orange or at the subsidiary electrical businesses that he supervised.
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_Goodrich_Acheson" title="Edward Goodrich Acheson">
        Edward Goodrich Acheson
       </a>
       - chemist, worked at Menlo Park 1880-1884
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/William_Symes_Andrews" title="William Symes Andrews">
        William Symes Andrews
       </a>
       - started at the Menlo Park machine shop 1879
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Batchelor" title="Charles Batchelor">
        Charles Batchelor
       </a>
       - "chief experimental assistant"
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/John_I._Beggs" title="John I. Beggs">
        John I. Beggs
       </a>
       - manager of
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Illuminating_Company" title="Edison Illuminating Company">
        Edison Illuminating Company
       </a>
       in New York, 1886
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/William_Kennedy_Dickson" title="William Kennedy Dickson">
        William Kennedy Dickson
       </a>
       - joined Menlo Park in 1823, worked on the motion picture camera
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Justus_B._Entz" title="Justus B. Entz">
        Justus B. Entz
       </a>
       - joined
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">
        Edison Machine Works
       </a>
       in 1887
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Fessenden" title="Reginald Fessenden">
        Reginald Fessenden
       </a>
       - worked at the
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">
        Edison Machine Works
       </a>
       in 1886
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">
        Henry Ford
       </a>
       - engineer
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Illuminating_Company" title="Edison Illuminating Company">
        Edison Illuminating Company
       </a>
       Detroit, Michigan, 1891-1899
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/William_Joseph_Hammer" title="William Joseph Hammer">
        William Joseph Hammer
       </a>
       - started as laboratory assistant Menlo Park in 1879
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Miller_Reese_Hutchison" title="Miller Reese Hutchison">
        Miller Reese Hutchison
       </a>
       - inventor of hearing aid
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hibberd_Johnson" title="Edward Hibberd Johnson">
        Edward Hibberd Johnson
       </a>
       - started in 1909, chief engineer at West Orange laboratory 1912-1918
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Insull" title="Samuel Insull">
        Samuel Insull
       </a>
       - started in 1881, rose to become VP of General Electric (1892) then President of Chicago Edison
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Kunihiko_Iwadare" title="Kunihiko Iwadare">
        Kunihiko Iwadare
       </a>
       - joined
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">
        Edison Machine Works
       </a>
       in 1887
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Francis_Jehl" title="Francis Jehl">
        Francis Jehl
       </a>
       - laboratory assistant Menlo Park 1879-1882
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Arthur_E._Kennelly" title="Arthur E. Kennelly">
        Arthur E. Kennelly
       </a>
       - engineer, experimentalist at West Orange laboratory 1887-1894
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/John_Kruesi" title="John Kruesi">
        John Kruesi
       </a>
       - started 1872, was head machinist, at Newark, Menlo Park,
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">
        Edison Machine Works
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Howard_Latimer" title="Lewis Howard Latimer">
        Lewis Howard Latimer
       </a>
       - hired 1884 as a draftsman, continued working for General Electric
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/John_W._Lieb" title="John W. Lieb">
        John W. Lieb
       </a>
       - worked at the
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">
        Edison Machine Works
       </a>
       in 1881
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Commerford_Martin" title="Thomas Commerford Martin">
        Thomas Commerford Martin
       </a>
       - electrical engineer, worked at Menlo Park 1877–1879
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/George_F._Morrison" title="George F. Morrison">
        George F. Morrison
       </a>
       - started at Edison Lamp Works 1882
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton_Porter" title="Edwin Stanton Porter">
        Edwin Stanton Porter
       </a>
       - joined the
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Manufacturing_Company" title="Edison Manufacturing Company">
        Edison Manufacturing Company
       </a>
       1899
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Frank_J._Sprague" title="Frank J. Sprague">
        Frank J. Sprague
       </a>
       - Joined Menlo Park 1883, became known as the "Father of Electric Traction".
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">
        Nikola Tesla
       </a>
       - electrical engineer and inventor, worked at the
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Machine_Works" title="Edison Machine Works">
        Edison Machine Works
       </a>
       in 1884
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Francis_Robbins_Upton" title="Francis Robbins Upton">
        Francis Robbins Upton
       </a>
       - mathematician/physicist, joined Menlo Park 1878
      </li>
     </ul>

          "Older Son To Sue To Void Edison Will; William, Second Child Of The Counsel".
          <i>
           New York Times
          </i>
          . October 31, 1931.
          <q>
           The will of Thomas A. Edison, filed in Newark last Thursday, which leaves the bulk of the inventor's $12 million estate to the sons of his second wife, was attacked as unfair yesterday by William L. Edison, second son of the first wife, who announced at the same time that he would sue to break it.

          </i>

           <a href="/wiki/West_Orange,_New_Jersey" title="West Orange, New Jersey">
            West Orange, New Jersey
           </a>
           , Sunday, October 18, 1931. Thomas Alva Edison died at 3:24 o'clock this morning at his home, Glenmont, in the Llewellyn Park section of this city. The great inventor, the fruits of whose genius so magically transformed the everyday world, was 84 years and 8 months old.

       <li id="cite_note-94">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-94">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation news">
          "
          <span style="padding-left:0.2em;">
           "
          </span>
          No Immortality of the Soul" says Thomas A. Edison. In Fact, He Doesn't Believe There Is a Soul — Human Beings Only an Aggregate of Cells and the Brain Only a Wonderful Machine, Says Wizard of Electricity".
          <i>
           New York Times
          </i>
          . October 2, 1910.
          <q>
           Thomas A. Edison in the following interview for the first time speaks to the public on the vital subjects of the human soul and immortality. It will be bound to be a most fascinating, an amazing statement, from one of the most notable and interesting men of the age ... Nature is what we know. We do not know the gods of religions. And nature is not kind, or merciful, or loving. If God made me — the fabled God of the three qualities of which I spoke: mercy, kindness, love — He also made the fish I catch and eat. And where do His mercy, kindness, and love for that fish come in? No; nature made us — nature did it all — not the gods of the religions.
          </q>
         </cite>
